  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_aa7d5a06-ebd5-4d78-a5e0-89e462c3dcfa'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer15341752603502967552">For more than two years, one book has taken over Germany's hardcover and paperback bestseller lists, reaching number one in Der Spiegel and setting off a frenzy in bookstores: <i>The Swarm</i>.<br/><br/>Whales begin sinking ships. Toxic, eyeless crabs poison Long Island's water supply. The North Sea shelf collapses, killing thousands in Europe. Around the world, countries are beginning to </span>
  <span id="freeText15341752603502967552" style="display:none">For more than two years, one book has taken over Germany's hardcover and paperback bestseller lists, reaching number one in Der Spiegel and setting off a frenzy in bookstores: <i>The Swarm</i>.<br /><br />Whales begin sinking ships. Toxic, eyeless crabs poison Long Island's water supply. The North Sea shelf collapses, killing thousands in Europe. Around the world, countries are beginning to feel the effects of the ocean's revenge as the seas and their inhabi-tants begin a violent revolution against mankind. In this riveting novel, full of twists, turns, and cliffhangers, a team of scientists discovers a strange, intelligent life force called the Yrr that takes form in marine animals, using them to wreak havoc on humanity for our ecological abuses. Soon a struggle between good and evil is in full swing, with both human and suboceanic forces battling for control of the waters. At stake is the survival of the Earth's fragile ecology -- and ultimately, the survival of the human race itself.<br /><br />The apocalyptic catastrophes of <em>The Day After Tomorrow</em> meet the watery menace of <em>The Abyss</em> in this gripping, scientifically realistic, and utterly imaginative thriller. With 1.5 million copies sold in Germany -- where it has been on the bestseller list without fail since its debut -- and the author's skillfully executed blend of compelling story, vivid characters, and eerie locales, Frank Schatzing's The Swarm will keep you in tense anticipation until the last suspenseful page is turned.<br />(front flap)</span>
  <a data-text-id="15341752603502967552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_6007102" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6007102" />
    <a title="Jacob Jones-Goldstein" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/368926-jacob-jones-goldstein"><img alt="Jacob Jones-Goldstein" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1189470962p2/368926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6007102?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Jones-Goldstein" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Jones-Goldstein" href="/user/show/368926-jacob-jones-goldstein">Jacob Jones-Goldstein</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/368926-jacob-jones-goldstein?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6007102" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12452318627327389182">This is probably the only non-political book where I wanted to punch the author in the face after reading it. Possibly the most irritating book I have read since The Dark Tower. the book is fine for about 600 pages, then Frank loses it completely. <br/><br/>First off, if after 600 pages you don't care whether a single character lives or dies, you know your in trouble. Secondly, if you are going to make the argument that everyone is just a mass of DNA and everything we do is the sole outcome of that DNA, w</span>
  <span id="freeText12452318627327389182" style="display:none">This is probably the only non-political book where I wanted to punch the author in the face after reading it. Possibly the most irritating book I have read since The Dark Tower. the book is fine for about 600 pages, then Frank loses it completely. <br /><br />First off, if after 600 pages you don't care whether a single character lives or dies, you know your in trouble. Secondly, if you are going to make the argument that everyone is just a mass of DNA and everything we do is the sole outcome of that DNA, why is every single American in this book practically evil? I don't have a problem with the portrayal as such, just that it doesn't jive with what he is putting forth about humanity. <br /><br />And Lastly, if the Yrr are so much better than humanity, so much more in tune with nature and basically benevolent, why are they using whales as projectiles? All of their offensives essentially involve that mass destruction of living creatures both human and non. That kind of hurts the case of them being benevolent avatars of mother nature or whatever he is trying to suggest. <br /><br />The chapter about the trip a particle takes through the oceans right in the middle of the books Climax was particularly galling. <br /><br />This may be the first any only time I invested this much time in a book to basically skim the ending. <br /><br />It is big and long so it would undoubtedly make excellent kindling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12452318627327389182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2215278754" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2215278754" />
    <a title="Fran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74816651-fran"><img alt="Fran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586773357p2/74816651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2215278754?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fran" href="/user/show/74816651-fran">Fran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2215278754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12939238283683987460">Speculative fiction at it's very best, and if the genre in which it has been categorized doesn't make you curious perhaps nothing will, for this is an apocalyptic eco-thriller, focused on telling the story of who is behind a series of catastrophic natural events occurring all too close together to be coincidental. <br/><br/>I loved this book. Not only is the science behind it solid (the author even spent some time sampling the gelid waters of the Northern Seas along a bunch of scientist) but the story is </span>
  <span id="freeText12939238283683987460" style="display:none">Speculative fiction at it's very best, and if the genre in which it has been categorized doesn't make you curious perhaps nothing will, for this is an apocalyptic eco-thriller, focused on telling the story of who is behind a series of catastrophic natural events occurring all too close together to be coincidental. <br /><br />I loved this book. Not only is the science behind it solid (the author even spent some time sampling the gelid waters of the Northern Seas along a bunch of scientist) but the story is compelling, and scary, and you can't stop reading. I think I finished the book in three days (and the thing is about 900 pages long) because I had to know how everything would end. <br /><br />The cast of characters is huge, diverse, and beyond wonderful. The majority of them are marine scientists from various fields, called into action when strange things start to happen in the world‚Äôs oceans. It all begins with the discovery of a new species of worms living on the methane hydrate deposits on the world‚Äôs deep-sea continental shelves. The worms feed on the methane deposits, weakening the continental shelves that soon start to collapse. But that's just the beginning, and when poisonous jellyfish and crabs come out from the sea to attack everything moving on land, whales start sinking ships, and underwater probes start going missing, it becomes obvious that there‚Äôs something going on, and that mankind has a new enemy.<br /><br />If you want to read a good thriller that is original beyond bounds, this is the book you have to choose because the novel works well. The premise is quite original and well researched. The book is entertaining, outdoing many Hollywood disaster movies in certain passages and it's a pleasurable read. Especially if you, too, sometimes wish the ocean could fight back.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_299277768" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/299277768" />
    <a title="Kirschkuchen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8346121-kirschkuchen"><img alt="Kirschkuchen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/299277768?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirschkuchen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirschkuchen" href="/user/show/8346121-kirschkuchen">Kirschkuchen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer299277768" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13458717077341371407">Sigur Johanson is a wormologist (He's actually a biologist at the university Trontheim and an expert for worms). Accidentally, there's a strange species of deep sea worms that eat a lot of stuff with complicated names. Things threaten to collapse on a continental level. Basically, the world as we know it will crumble under the impact of worms (so far so awesome).<br/><br/>Leon Anawak is a whalist (he's a maritime scientist and studies whale behaviour) and is taken aback when he watches whales attack boats</span>
  <span id="freeText13458717077341371407" style="display:none">Sigur Johanson is a wormologist (He's actually a biologist at the university Trontheim and an expert for worms). Accidentally, there's a strange species of deep sea worms that eat a lot of stuff with complicated names. Things threaten to collapse on a continental level. Basically, the world as we know it will crumble under the impact of worms (so far so awesome).<br /><br />Leon Anawak is a whalist (he's a maritime scientist and studies whale behaviour) and is taken aback when he watches whales attack boats and kill people. *gasp*<br /><br />More bad things happen: jellyfish kill people, ships collide, there's a suspicious absence of the Gulf Stream, a massive crab attack, an epidemic in France (because of exploding lobsters) and - I almost forgot - a tsunami that kills a few million people and destroys most cities close to the Baltic Sea (the worms!!! Where's Kevin Bacon when you need him?). <br /><br />Tina Lund dies (This is the first in a serious of unrelated characters doing unrelated things with no impact on the main plot (I lied. It's not the first, but at one point I stopped counting).<br /><br />Naturally, all events are related. After what feels like 500 pages, the world is in shambles.<br /><br />Even though most of the damage has been done in Europe and the main protagonist is from Europe, the Americans start a task force (they kidnap every scientist and order them to... be scientists).<br /><br />They all go to a castle in the mountains for research. After all the above mentioned coordinated attacks that have picked up speed, culminating in the Tsunami and forcing the world to react as a unity, what do you think happens? Exactly! Nothing. For excruciating months and about 800 pages, nothing really happens. This is because- Oh, look! An unrelated character is doing something unrelated! Shiny!<br /><br />The attacks stop and there's research going on. Kind of. Actually, everyone does a little bit of puttering around.<br /><br />Johanson, who's not only a wormologist but a strange-thingologist, develops the following theory (we instantly know that he's right): There's an unknown species living in the depths of the ocean since the beginning of time. They are doing things because- Oh, look! An unrelated character is doing something unrelated! Shiny! <br /><br />The Yrr (just for the record, I think that's a stupid name) are single-cell organisms and operate like tiny Borg (--&gt; The Star Trek works because the book swings the moral bat worse than Captain Picard, sadly without having any of his boyish charm).<br /><br />Then there's a 400 page description of someone's father's funeral.<br /><br />Then everyone goes on a ship.<br /><br />Let's just think about this for a moment. There's something in the ocean that makes every creature in it go wonky, and that has the power to stop the gulf stream. Is it reasonable to put every scientist who has even the slightest chance to fight back on the same ship and let them sail off into the deep sea? Obviously it is, because the book expects you to believe this is a good idea.<br /><br />There's a secret lab that we know must be there when we read the word 'ship' for the first time, but we need about 300 pages to discover it. Then we need another 600 pages to get inside the lab, and really, it's unexciting.<br /><br />I feel I should mention the evil American antagonist with Asian background, General Li (American Navi General, think about it for a moment). General Li (navy) is such an outrageously clich√©d villain, though, that I refuse to admit her existence.<br /><br />The person representing the United States (the one I don't want to discuss), wants to kill all Yrr, and she knows how to do it. Somehow we're made to believe that killing a species who's determined to erase mankind and destroys half of the earth while doing so is a bad idea. <br /><br />Instead wormo-thingologist Johanson, develops a communication method that involves a dead man, Yrr-pheromones, mini submarines and blowing himself up in the middle of a tremendously boring 800 pages showdown. Don't ask - only MacGyver can make that explanation sound reasonable.<br /><br />BTW, if you ever asked yourself how you can possibly make trained military dolphins boring, read the book! It's a great step by step guide.<br /><br />Then we finally realise, why during the last 2,000 pages, we've constantly read about Karen Weaver. You don't need to know anything about Karen Weaver except that she was somehow around, is the token female character and love interest of... uh... someone. She is now saving the world after Johanson's (and navy general's) death. Obviously she does that by kidnapping the already mentioned dead body with a mini submarine. While she remains in the boat, the body bravely jumps into the water to communicate with the Yrr. HUSSAH!<br /><br />The attacks of the Yrr stop then, because the dead body which is full of their natural pheromones and... Alright, I give up.<br /><br />The book ends.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13458717077341371407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Chris_P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p"><img alt="Chris_P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558554241p2/44309339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1708470880?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris_P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris_P" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p">Chris_P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44309339-chris-p?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1708470880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2358443264388216257">From the first time I laid eyes on the plot of <i>The Swarm</i>, I knew I had to read it. Nature taking revenge through marine animals for all the shit man has caused. Whales killing people, worms coordinating their efforts to cause tsunamis, mutant crabs spreading a deadly virus and so on... I was imagining <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uCEeAn6_QJo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tool's √Ünema</a> in literary form. Now, I'm left wondering how such a good idea can end up so fucked up...<br/><br/>Well, the answer is rather simple. The greek edition is 1018 pages long. Every 20-30 pages of p</span>
  <span id="freeText2358443264388216257" style="display:none">From the first time I laid eyes on the plot of <i>The Swarm</i>, I knew I had to read it. Nature taking revenge through marine animals for all the shit man has caused. Whales killing people, worms coordinating their efforts to cause tsunamis, mutant crabs spreading a deadly virus and so on... I was imagining <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uCEeAn6_QJo" rel="nofollow noopener">Tool's √Ünema</a> in literary form. Now, I'm left wondering how such a good idea can end up so fucked up...<br /><br />Well, the answer is rather simple. The greek edition is 1018 pages long. Every 20-30 pages of plot development and rudimentary suspense were followed by 40-50 pages where characters were doing nothing important at all. Not to mention a couple of 50-page-long breaks where everything that happens is totally unrelated to the story. What's more, the endless blabbering of the omniscient narrator about scientific facts tested my patience any number of times. At first I found those facts to be interesting and educational but soon they became tiring and totally distracting of the plot which took what seemed like forever to develop.<br /><br />As if all this wasn't enough, I found the dialogs to be a bit unrealistic. It was like the author was struggling with them and forcing himself to write them in order to make his characters more familiar to the reader. Although, I'll give him the benefit of the doubt on this as it may well have been the translator's* fault.<br /><br />What I found a bit fun, if cheap, was the fact that it's the first among all books and movies about the end of the world where the Americans are the psychotic lunatics who are willing, in their stupidity, to risk sending the world to hell in order to exploit what's happening for their own benefit. Of course this makes sense as the author is German but, if only for a little while, I enjoyed the originality of the notion. On the long run, though, portraying the Americans in such a caricatural manner served only negatively.<br /><br /><i>The Swarm</i> had the potential to be a great novel and that made me even angrier. Never mind if I don't like a book. It happens and I'm ok with it. But when I come across a book that not only has a very interesting story to tell but also has a few great elements (like the idea of the final solution which I found magnificent), the last thing I'd like to see it do is waste its own potential. It's a shame but that's exactly how I felt about <i>The Swarm</i>. If only it was 600 pages long instead of 1000 and included more action and substance instead of an ocean of useless information, I'm sure it would be one of the greatest novels of the previous decade.<br /><br />*ŒíœÅŒÆŒ∫Œ± Œ≥ŒµŒªŒøŒØŒø œÑŒø Œ≥ŒµŒ≥ŒøŒΩœåœÇ œåœÑŒπ œáœÅŒ∑œÉŒπŒºŒøœÄŒøŒπŒµŒØœÑŒ±Œπ œÉŒµ œåŒªŒø œÑŒø Œ≤ŒπŒ≤ŒªŒØŒø œÄŒªŒ∑Œ∏œÖŒΩœÑŒπŒ∫œåœÇ ŒµœÖŒ≥ŒµŒΩŒµŒØŒ±œÇ Œ±ŒΩŒ¨ŒºŒµœÉŒ± œÉœÑŒøœÖœÇ œáŒ±œÅŒ±Œ∫œÑŒÆœÅŒµœÇ. <span id="freeTextContainer10105810835733043220">This is by far one of the <b>most amazing</b> books I have ever read in my life. First off, the plot is beyond epic. For as long as we can remember, we have always thought about the existence of aliens in outer space, but we rarely thought about them being underwater. This is where Schatzing does his magic and really gets you going. Many of you know the famous line:<br/><i>We know more about space than we know about our own oceans</i><br/>Well, this statement is out into action after <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[the discovery of the</span></span>
  <span id="freeText10105810835733043220" style="display:none">This is by far one of the <b>most amazing</b> books I have ever read in my life. First off, the plot is beyond epic. For as long as we can remember, we have always thought about the existence of aliens in outer space, but we rarely thought about them being underwater. This is where Schatzing does his magic and really gets you going. Many of you know the famous line:<br /><i>We know more about space than we know about our own oceans</i><br />Well, this statement is out into action after <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the discovery of the Yrr <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />Secondly, I feel obliged to compliment the author's writing style. I could swear that he made me itch to turn the page at the end of each chapter. His style is suspenseful, logical and very fast-paced. You won't have a single moment of rest with this book, that is guaranteed.<br />Lastly, I would like to give all readers an important piece of advice regarding this book: <br /><u>
  <b>DO NOT, I REPEAT,DO NOT TAKE THIS BOOK WITH YOU TO THE BEACH, AS YOU WILL COME TO REGRET IT LIKE I HAVE</b>
</u><br /><br />A five star-rating is in order here....Well done, Frank..</span>

<div id="review_98869979" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98869979" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98869979?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340895245" href="/book/show/1864747.The_Swarm">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2008-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=suspense-thriller">suspense-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=award-winning">award-winning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=environment">environment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=challenging">challenging</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98869979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8185425437722733009">Nature strikes back. This is the most important message of this book: we have to take care of our environment and that definitely includes the sea, the ocean. In this wonderful sci-fi book, a group of scientists have to fight against the <b>Yrr</b>, an alien kind of one-celled microorganisms found in the bottom of the sea that have the ability to eat away the continental drift and when these pesky creatures destroy the drift, it causes tsunami and flooding. These Yrr can even pollute small crabs and lo</span>
  <span id="freeText8185425437722733009" style="display:none">Nature strikes back. This is the most important message of this book: we have to take care of our environment and that definitely includes the sea, the ocean. In this wonderful sci-fi book, a group of scientists have to fight against the <b>Yrr</b>, an alien kind of one-celled microorganisms found in the bottom of the sea that have the ability to eat away the continental drift and when these pesky creatures destroy the drift, it causes tsunami and flooding. These Yrr can even pollute small crabs and lobsters and when these delicious creatures are eaten in New York, it causes epidemic of wide magnitude. Red tide, right? But Schatzing did not use that term here. These Yrr can also trigger the mutation of mussels (I am not kidding) so they become highly mobile and attack whaling ships. These small microorganisms can also be sniffed by whales and orcas that can make these huge sea animals to get crazy and turn ocean liners upside down and sink into the bottom of the sea.<br /><br />The first time this book was published in Germany in 1998, it stayed in their bestsellers' list for 7 years. This English translation came out in the US in 2004. Hollywood actress and producer Uma Thurman and the German producers bought this book's film rights. On May 9, 2007, it was announced that the film was in the works. Ted Tally has finished writing the screenplay and Dino De Laurentiis was to be one of the prime financial benefactors of the project. The release date is estimated to be in <b>2015.</b> (Source: Wiki). Eight years to make a huge movie out of this well-written sci-fi. It should be a MUST see movie!<br /><br />I say that this is a well-written sci-fi because its mind-boggling claims are backed by scientific explanations. What I mean is that Schatzing really did his research (he is a marine biologist by profession) while writing the book and so each of what the Yrr can do is well-explained to make the whole thing plausible to happen. Each sub-plot - chains and chains of tragedies happening in the different parts of the world - is well-structured and well-developed and reading it felt like you are watching different cable channels announcing different sea-related incidents and catastrophes happening if not at the same time, close to each other. The whole book leaves a scary tone but it is a definitely a wake up call for all of us. That's why this probably woke up German readers to put this book into their top spot of their bestsellers' list for 7 years.<br /><br />The only little criticism that I'd like to point out is that the characters seem like bystanders in this whole thing. What I mean is that if you are fond of reading character-driven plots, this book is not really for you. I mean, by nature sci-fi are event-driven but some great authors still able to incorporate well-developed characters into event-driven plots like Ray Bradbury's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4381.Fahrenheit_451" title="Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury" rel="nofollow noopener">Fahrenheit 451</a> or Walter M. Miller, Jr.'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/164154.A_Canticle_for_Leibowitz" title="A Canticle for Leibowitz by Walter M. Miller Jr." rel="nofollow noopener">A Canticle for Leibowitz</a>, the last sci-fi I read prior to this. Both books have mind-boggling premise but the characters are involved in those and they "evolve" as the plot progresses.<br /><br />Also, caveat should be given to those sci-fi aficionados who read sci-fi <i>only</i> for entertainment. You might find readng some scientific details boring. This is the reason why it took me too many days to finish this book. But if you also read sci-fi to further your scientific knowledge on the matters that the author included in the book, then I promise that you will not get bored at all with this Schatzing's masterpiece. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="8185425437722733009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_94146773" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94146773" />
    <a title="Gudmundur" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3439823-gudmundur"><img alt="Gudmundur" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344869638p2/3439823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94146773?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gudmundur" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gudmundur" href="/user/show/3439823-gudmundur">Gudmundur</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94146773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2181166252724645196">Pretty good yarn if not taken too seriously. The strengths are that the story is obviously well researched and the biological and scientific explanations given look quite convincing (although my knowledge of the field is admittedly limited). The "enemy" is interesting and adequately mind-boggling for a sci-fi yarn. The cons: The characterization is pretty weak, and some of the characters are far too cartoonish to be convincing (Judith Li, Greywolf...); the technique of the all knowing narrator i</span>
  <span id="freeText2181166252724645196" style="display:none">Pretty good yarn if not taken too seriously. The strengths are that the story is obviously well researched and the biological and scientific explanations given look quite convincing (although my knowledge of the field is admittedly limited). The "enemy" is interesting and adequately mind-boggling for a sci-fi yarn. The cons: The characterization is pretty weak, and some of the characters are far too cartoonish to be convincing (Judith Li, Greywolf...); the technique of the all knowing narrator is used a bit haphazardly (and when the narrator "peaked" into the mind of Peak, what came out was pretty stereotypical and at least once down right racist); the author really likes to show that he did all this research, and there are lengthy chapters and monologues with scientific details that drag things down; when the author goes on existential sprees I lost patience and just skipped the pages (Karens descent down to the bottom of the Arctic sea, f.ex., went on forever with existential musings and halucinations that did not add anything to what was happening). And the showdown aboard the Independence was quite silly, indeed the authors treatment of the americans (although understandably sarcastic, given the flood of movies and books where the Americans come and save the day and turn 4th of July into the Independence day of the world) was far too cartoonish and silly and really dragged the whole thing down, especially for a novel trying to go for scientific veracity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2181166252724645196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74632311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6650577091938468598">A great plot idea by a terrible author. Not recommended. Characters are flat, one-dimensional, and predictable, at times the book is downright sexist and, yes, racist. Language (I read the German original) is puerile and often vulgar in a dumb, embarrassing way. While some of the science in the book appears plausible to me, other aspects are strangely under-researched: Who ever heard of the rank of "general commander" in the US Navy? The absolute low is when Sch√§tzing descends into the depths of</span>
  <span id="freeText6650577091938468598" style="display:none">A great plot idea by a terrible author. Not recommended. Characters are flat, one-dimensional, and predictable, at times the book is downright sexist and, yes, racist. Language (I read the German original) is puerile and often vulgar in a dumb, embarrassing way. While some of the science in the book appears plausible to me, other aspects are strangely under-researched: Who ever heard of the rank of "general commander" in the US Navy? The absolute low is when Sch√§tzing descends into the depths of pseudo-philosophical bullshit. Which he often does. <br /><br />I guess I shouldn't be too harsh on a book that kept me going through some 1300 pages. So I'll give it one thing: The plot was exciting enough to keep me laboring through to the end. <br /><br />I need to start reading real books again.</span>

      1001 Books Group Read
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2493714-cindy?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2493714-cindy?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87696258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16179173900389595943">I'm wavering between 2 and 3 stars for this Crichton-esque brick of a sea-thriller.<br/><br/>On one hand you have whales, crabs, dolphins, sea worms, shoals, and sharks galore. All awesome. Oh and the top fru-fru Parisian restaurant infested with gooey lobsters. Right on. Also, there's some interesting thoughts on life-forms, consciousness, collectives and intelligence. I'll be thinking about those ideas for a while, even if they aren't anything new. The thriller and horror part of the story was plenty in</span>
  <span id="freeText16179173900389595943" style="display:none">I'm wavering between 2 and 3 stars for this Crichton-esque brick of a sea-thriller.<br /><br />On one hand you have whales, crabs, dolphins, sea worms, shoals, and sharks galore. All awesome. Oh and the top fru-fru Parisian restaurant infested with gooey lobsters. Right on. Also, there's some interesting thoughts on life-forms, consciousness, collectives and intelligence. I'll be thinking about those ideas for a while, even if they aren't anything new. The thriller and horror part of the story was plenty interesting to keep me turning the pages quickly.<br /><br />On the other, much angrier sullied hand, you have obscene amounts of political, environmental and philosophical/religious pontification, aimed both directly at the reader and through the super-genius-beautiful-yet-one-dimensional cast of characters. Gah. I was so looking forward to a scientific thriller in translation that presented a multi-national cast and world view. The Swarm has this in spades, but also is incredibly one-sided in its vehement anti-US stance. The rest of the world is beautiful, intelligent, and rational. The US, however, is the source of all the world's woes, including <i>inventing</i> conspiracy theories?! I have lived for over 4 years in other countries, and it's dead easy to vilify the US. But the thing is <i>all</i> countries and their citizens have both bad and good virtues. No one is perfect, just like no country as a whole is pure evil. To portray the US as the latter is lazy characterization, an cheap shot and frankly just immature.<br /><br />Finally, last gripe: the science is beyond sketchy. Sch√§tzing clearly has done a ton of research (he packed every last detail of it in the book - that's why it's over 800 pages!). Yet he gets so much of the science wrong. Methane does not smell like rotten eggs - it's odorless.<br /><br />I really like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85873189" rel="nofollow noopener">Jay's review</a> that said that:<br />The Swarm:Science :: Da Vinci Code:Religion<br /><br />That pretty much sums it up. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16179173900389595943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6839765156893931130"><b>3.5 stars rounded up to 4!</b> <br/><br/><i>The Swarm by Frank Sch√§tzing</i> has been on my TBR for quite a while and I am very happy that I have finally given this book a try. <b>I liked the blurb</b> and the premise of nature rebelling against how it is misstreated by humanity. Wales, worms and other animals are apparently working together in order to put a stop to the human infraction of nature. <br/><br/>I definitley <b>liked the whole concept of the story</b> and the fact that dangerous things are happening at different places all aro</span>
  <span id="freeText6839765156893931130" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars rounded up to 4!</b> <br /><br /><i>The Swarm by Frank Sch√§tzing</i> has been on my TBR for quite a while and I am very happy that I have finally given this book a try. <b>I liked the blurb</b> and the premise of nature rebelling against how it is misstreated by humanity. Wales, worms and other animals are apparently working together in order to put a stop to the human infraction of nature. <br /><br />I definitley <b>liked the whole concept of the story</b> and the fact that dangerous things are happening at different places all around the world. Scientists, investors and other characters from different countries are quickly starting to work together to put an end to the nature's rebellion. The book is <b>very informative from a scientific point of few</b> - the <b>creativity of the author is remarkable</b>, but I unfortunately had the feeling that many chapters of the story have been a little too long and too full of information. Parts of the story read like a science book and I had to force myself to not skim these pages. <br /><br />And the major aspect I want to critisize is the fact that <b>the many characters invovled in finding a solution to the problem</b> felt a little too flat at places. I wasn't able to connect with most of them and I generally had the feeling that the author was focusing too strongly on the scientific part of the books, while forgetting the wide array of possible human reactions to the very dangerous situation. <br /><br />I am glad that I gave this book a try and the general topic and the author's creativity have made it a unique read - but as mentioned above, the book does have <b>quite a few weaknesses!</b> </span>
  <a data-text-id="6839765156893931130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8922649899595369975">Sch√§tzing has done a great amount of research for this, inquiring at actual marine biology institutes and other knowledge centres. If you look around a bit, you will note that his information is still up to date.<br/><br/>He manages to tread the fine line between too much information and superficiality very well.   <span id="freeText8922649899595369975" style="display:none">Sch√§tzing has done a great amount of research for this, inquiring at actual marine biology institutes and other knowledge centres. If you look around a bit, you will note that his information is still up to date.<br /><br />He manages to tread the fine line between too much information and superficiality very well. He makes accessible scientific information to a lay audience, which might be the most praiseworthy author's activity there is. I'd be grateful if he could write a book like this on every other subject as well. <br /><br />The book starts out as a thriller, reinvents itself as science book, and, suddenly, somewhere past the middle, Sch√§tzing catches that tone that is more than just competent writing. Though he has been smooth and witty from the beginning, he discovers a touch of brightness halfway through.<br /><br />I love this: it is a book that touches everything.<br /><br />Beware of cover texts on foreign editions: often they give away the plot for about 3/4 of the book.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12525481260413476766">I was yearning for a scifi story with a focus on a water based species and ended up reading The Swarm. It was everything I wanted and more. Though not all of the 'more' was enjoyable.<br/><br/>There's a lot of science in this that I assume is accurate based on the long list of scientists thanked at the books end. Coupled with the writing style, the story comes across quite cold and calculated, but still enjoyable provided that a negative opinion of our species impact on the planet doesn't upset you. There</span>
  <span id="freeText12525481260413476766" style="display:none">I was yearning for a scifi story with a focus on a water based species and ended up reading The Swarm. It was everything I wanted and more. Though not all of the 'more' was enjoyable.<br /><br />There's a lot of science in this that I assume is accurate based on the long list of scientists thanked at the books end. Coupled with the writing style, the story comes across quite cold and calculated, but still enjoyable provided that a negative opinion of our species impact on the planet doesn't upset you. There's quite a bit cack handed moralising but if you share the authors politics it won't bother you much. <br /><br />Definitely one for those that consider themselves thinkers rather than feelers.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/90767-elvedril?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/90767-elvedril?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1328065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6981578238809986769">A well written thriller with a very interesting plot. Despite its strengths it is held back by an unlikely premise and the author's inability to stop pontificating about international politics through his characters. <br/><br/>It reads like a bit like a multi-writer Mary Sue story. I don't mean the fact that pretty much every important character is a certifiable genius, that's fine since the story revolves around the top experts in multiple fields working together. Okay, that's wrong, it's not fine. It's </span>
  <span id="freeText6981578238809986769" style="display:none">A well written thriller with a very interesting plot. Despite its strengths it is held back by an unlikely premise and the author's inability to stop pontificating about international politics through his characters. <br /><br />It reads like a bit like a multi-writer Mary Sue story. I don't mean the fact that pretty much every important character is a certifiable genius, that's fine since the story revolves around the top experts in multiple fields working together. Okay, that's wrong, it's not fine. It's really annoying to read long descriptions of just how wonderfully gifted and intelligent everybody is, and how much they respect each other's brilliant insights in their fields of knowledge, but it's understandable as part of the plot. The part that really got me is the melodrama in character backgrounds. Like I said, all geniuses, but nobody from a standard family background in the lot. Everybody was orphaned at a young age, grew up on some sort of metaphorical or literal "mean streets", or been touched by a dark government conspiracy. And don't get me started on the need to describe just how amazingly charming and attractive some of them are. Or the exaggerated evil of every American in the book. Is that really necessary to make the characters interesting? I find it hard to believe that you can get 20 top scientists in a room and not find one science nerd from the suburbs who worked hard and went to a good school. I'm honestly surprised none of the characters discovered they were the lost heirs to magical kingdoms...<br /><br />Furthermore, I found the science aspect of the science fiction to be somewhat unconvincing. Though it could be very accurate for all I know, since my background in biology isn't very strong. The author thanks a long list of people with PhDs for their help so it's possible. Then again if you can't write real science in a way that's believable... Eh, that's not fair, fact is sometimes stranger than fiction.<br /><br />Finally, I found the last 40 pages or so to be really frustrating. It was basically all pontification. It was like reading an empiricist revenge fantasy. A German patriot, environmentalist, empiricist revenge fantasy. It seemed like a weak payoff for 800 pages of reading and makes it hard for me to recommend the book to anyone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6981578238809986769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1464034896983138601" style="display:none">After I read this book for the first time, it remained my favorite for half a year. I still enjoy going back and reading my favorite parts occasionally. On the other hand, it's really heavy. Difficult to curl up in bed with.<br /><br />The plot starts with several scientists scattered around the world who start to notice odd things about the ocean. The odd and disturbing occurrences build up until - bam - apocalypse. Actually, it's a small version of the apocalypse, but close enough.<br /><br />And the scientists gather into an intellectually elite conference to figure out how to keep the yrr - the intelligent alien organisms in the seas that have decided humans are causing too much environmental damage - from finishing them all off.<br /><br />I found the plot absolutely brilliant, and riveting enough to keep me wondering until the end. There was a lot of scientific/science fiction involved, which should appeal to anyone interested in oceanography.<br /><br />Unlike other reviewers, I didn't find the characters one-dimensional. I thought there were too many to keep track of, but I cared about what happened to each of them. I didn't notice each one being described as beautiful either, and certainly not often enough to make it a distraction.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer51382628572027174">An interesting mix of eco-thriller and "What if" and some kind of pre dystopian drama (yes, I'm having some troubles fitting this one into my genres/shelves ;)).<br/><br/>This book isn't perfect, the characters are not round enough for me to keep them all apart at all times, some kind of glossar/cast list would've been nice (or maybe that's just my edition?)   <span id="freeText51382628572027174" style="display:none">An interesting mix of eco-thriller and "What if" and some kind of pre dystopian drama (yes, I'm having some troubles fitting this one into my genres/shelves ;)).<br /><br />This book isn't perfect, the characters are not round enough for me to keep them all apart at all times, some kind of glossar/cast list would've been nice (or maybe that's just my edition?) Anyways, the book makes it up with an quite exciting plot that was fast paced for the most time. Not all of it - some scenes were a bit too long, too much, too repetitive - 800 pages or so might've been enough. But well, when it gets exciting, it does really, oh my. The horror scenarios were well executed and some of the images floating (haha, fitting) through my mind while reading the descritpions were extremely gruesome. Will Lobster ever be the same again? I have my doubts.<br /><br />Finally, I'm very much in awe when I think about the research this book must've taken. I'm no expert on any of the subjects presented here, but it all sounded logical (at least to me) and well explained. What a workload, wow. Respect, Herr Sch√§tzing.<br /><br />I think it's a must read, a contemporary classic. I took some time finishing it, but it was time well spent.</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1467832543" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer908329069285419627"><br/><br/><br/><br/>ŒîŒµŒØœÑŒµ œÑŒ∑ŒΩ Œ∫œÅŒπœÑŒπŒ∫ŒÆ ŒºŒµ ŒµŒπŒ∫œåŒΩŒµœÇ œÉœÑŒ± ŒïŒªŒªŒ∑ŒΩŒπŒ∫Œ¨ œÉœÑŒπœÇ <a href='https://kyriakosorokkou.wordpress.com/2020/05/23/%cf%84%ce%bf-%cf%83%ce%bc%ce%ae%ce%bd%ce%bf%cf%82-%ce%b7-%cf%8d%cf%83%cf%84%ce%b1%cf%84%ce%b7-%ce%b3%ce%b5%cf%81%ce%bf%ce%bd%cf%84%ce%bf%ce%ba%cf%8c%cf%81%ce%b7/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Œ≤ŒπŒ≤ŒªŒπŒøŒ±ŒªœáŒ∑ŒºŒµŒØŒµœÇ</a><br/><br/>This was the oldest book on my TBR (To Be Read) list.<br/>I bought it for myself in December 2007.<br/>I started reading it in January 2008, but that was the month when I began my university studies so I left the book aside.<br/><br/>Next year I went to Germany, the author's homeland, so I took it with me. But as always when I'm on holidays I enjoy the place I am in, travelling and sightseeing. So I abandoned it once more.<br/><br/>It had waited 10 more summers un</span>
  <span id="freeText908329069285419627" style="display:none"><br /><br /><br /><br />ŒîŒµŒØœÑŒµ œÑŒ∑ŒΩ Œ∫œÅŒπœÑŒπŒ∫ŒÆ ŒºŒµ ŒµŒπŒ∫œåŒΩŒµœÇ œÉœÑŒ± ŒïŒªŒªŒ∑ŒΩŒπŒ∫Œ¨ œÉœÑŒπœÇ <a href="https://kyriakosorokkou.wordpress.com/2020/05/23/%cf%84%ce%bf-%cf%83%ce%bc%ce%ae%ce%bd%ce%bf%cf%82-%ce%b7-%cf%8d%cf%83%cf%84%ce%b1%cf%84%ce%b7-%ce%b3%ce%b5%cf%81%ce%bf%ce%bd%cf%84%ce%bf%ce%ba%cf%8c%cf%81%ce%b7/" rel="nofollow noopener">Œ≤ŒπŒ≤ŒªŒπŒøŒ±ŒªœáŒ∑ŒºŒµŒØŒµœÇ</a><br /><br />This was the oldest book on my TBR (To Be Read) list.<br />I bought it for myself in December 2007.<br />I started reading it in January 2008, but that was the month when I began my university studies so I left the book aside.<br /><br />Next year I went to Germany, the author's homeland, so I took it with me. But as always when I'm on holidays I enjoy the place I am in, travelling and sightseeing. So I abandoned it once more.<br /><br />It had waited 10 more summers until I read it last June.<br /><br />Even though it's 1021 pages long it wasn't boring and slow paced; for my standards.<br /><br />It had a Crichtonian feeling (lots of science, adventure and (environmental) ethics)<br /><br />It's an eco-thriller, where nature takes revenge upon Man. Whales attack ships, crabs enter New York's sewer system, tsunamis destroy the shores of Northern Europe, and humans unprepared in their arrogance want to find a solution, and a reason for all this.<br /><br />A small negative is that the Americans and the British are the bad guys in a caricature-like way. Which in a way has some quanta of truth. <i>Divide and Conquer</i>, <i>God Bless America</i>, and similar mottoes. <br /><br />I enjoyed this book because it was my oldest from my TBR, my last I read for June, and I also enjoyed the story and it made me think:<br /><br />When will we stop destroying the environment we inherited?<br />How long will the Americans control with audacity the world?<br />and many more.<br /><br />It's not a masterpiece, but I had a good time reading it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="908329069285419627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Mita</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer85873189&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer85873189" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9234979910575800484">As much a hate to make the comparison, it begs to be drawn: The Swarm is to nature and science what The Da Vinci Code was to religion and history and art.<br/>I enjoyed this book from the start, and felt that the scientific rationale and research was well explained and clear without being condescending or watered down for the uninitiated - I never felt lost in jargon or bored by technicalities.<br/>While there most certainly is a message in the work I didn't feel like I was being hamer with (all 880 pages</span>
  <span id="freeText9234979910575800484" style="display:none">As much a hate to make the comparison, it begs to be drawn: The Swarm is to nature and science what The Da Vinci Code was to religion and history and art.<br />I enjoyed this book from the start, and felt that the scientific rationale and research was well explained and clear without being condescending or watered down for the uninitiated - I never felt lost in jargon or bored by technicalities.<br />While there most certainly is a message in the work I didn't feel like I was being hamer with (all 880 pages of) it. The book moved at a cracking pace and the author wasn't afraid to throw key characters to the sharks (quite literally).<br /></span>

<div id="review_2389364478" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2389364478" />
    <a title="Sabrina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31265489-sabrina"><img alt="Sabrina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427683864p2/31265489.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2389364478?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sabrina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sabrina" href="/user/show/31265489-sabrina">Sabrina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783596164530" href="/book/show/1168487.Der_Schwarm">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31265489-sabrina?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2389364478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15065120045746785389">I‚Äôm torn about this book. On the one hand, I want everyone to like and read it, because this Science Fiction story is written by a German author and widely read in the German-speaking community. I mean have you seen how many reviews this book has? And what a rating! On the other hand, now, that I‚Äôve read all 1000 pages of it (on a positive note, it might be shorter in English, Germans use a lot of words), I wouldn‚Äôt want to repeat the experience.<br/><br/>Let‚Äôs just summarise the positives and negatives, </span>
  <span id="freeText15065120045746785389" style="display:none">I‚Äôm torn about this book. On the one hand, I want everyone to like and read it, because this Science Fiction story is written by a German author and widely read in the German-speaking community. I mean have you seen how many reviews this book has? And what a rating! On the other hand, now, that I‚Äôve read all 1000 pages of it (on a positive note, it might be shorter in English, Germans use a lot of words), I wouldn‚Äôt want to repeat the experience.<br /><br />Let‚Äôs just summarise the positives and negatives, so you can decide by yourself:<br /><br />+ The setting: I love the ocean and its inhabitants. I enjoy diving and exploring the underwater. The author incorporated well researched facts that surprised, shocked or enlightened me: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ like the navy uses (used?) dolphins as spies and for combat  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />+ The thematic: how far can we go until we destroy our world? How much more of our waste can the oceans and climate bear? What happens when nature decides it‚Äôs too much and starts to fight back? For me the strongest point of the story was how the apocalypse slowly rolled through the pages and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and ended in an unimaginable tsunami that hits Europe destroying everything we know  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />+ The world-wide focus. In apocalyptic stories I often miss the world-wide attention. We usually know what happens in the States, but what about Europe, South America or Africa? How did it come about? How do the different countries react? This was still too superficial but came a little closer that previous stories.<br /><br />- The characters: one-dimensional, interchangeable and stereotype. For me a story lives with and through its characters. If I don‚Äôt care who lives and who dies you lost me. I think the only character I cared about was Johanson. One of the other main characters Anawak was just too annoying: I mean an academic who gets angry when people question him? Isn‚Äôt that the cornerstone of research worldwide? To search and to question? I also didn‚Äôt buy into the American military lady who primarily thought about <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ glory, world-dominion and how to fight the thread (never mind negation or the extinction of the human race).  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> This is oversimplification and stereotype.<br /><br />- The information load: while interesting at times it was way too much and sometimes not even fully relevant to the story <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ e.g. side-story about the Indians in Canada.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> While it seemed to be well researched. I read a lot of scientific articles for work and therefore a book is never about its information but about its experience to me. If you love interesting facts pulled into this story I urge you to give it a try.<br /><br />- The ending / solution: It sounds rather unbelievable. My colleagues at work, who recommended me this book said, that I shouldn‚Äôt be reading it before a dive, but I would jump into the water right now if I were near a beautiful spot‚Ä¶. <br /><br />For whom is this book? That‚Äôs really difficult, it is still well liked, so I guess if you like a story with facts give it a go.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15065120045746785389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1894023678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1827195217794219633">A year of big books. I am not slacking. But this is the second a-thousander (= a book of 1000+ pages, my creation. move along now pleb) I read this year. And probably another one follows so this year's challenge is doomed<br/><br/>So. I absolutely loved the concept. Underwater aliens, science and environmental issues: precisely my cup of tea. Pretty hard sci-fi. I had a few issues when genomics were involved but other than that, kudos for all the science facts. I actually learned quite a few things from t</span>
  <span id="freeText1827195217794219633" style="display:none">A year of big books. I am not slacking. But this is the second a-thousander (= a book of 1000+ pages, my creation. move along now pleb) I read this year. And probably another one follows so this year's challenge is doomed<br /><br />So. I absolutely loved the concept. Underwater aliens, science and environmental issues: precisely my cup of tea. Pretty hard sci-fi. I had a few issues when genomics were involved but other than that, kudos for all the science facts. I actually learned quite a few things from this read. Which is great. Including: fields of marine and benthic biology, global oceanic circulation, xenobiology, offshore oil refining, oceanic substrate and many more. Only non-science book I ever read that comes close to this was "Seveneves" (which tries to teach to teach the reader nuclear physics and orbital mechanics soooo yeah...).<br /><br />As for the downsides: I pretty much could not associate with ANY character in the book. They were nice enough, well written, lifelike etc, but I kind of disliked every single one of them for some reason. Especially the old narcissist professor guy. He may be a hero after all, but WHO THE HELL brings a goddamn basket full of expensive wines and cheeses on a scientific vessel while the world is ending. I mean, go seduce a student or something...<br /><br />Also, it could stand well enough if it was 300 pages shorter. <br /><br />Overall 4/5. Great read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1827195217794219633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9372583191935907733">If Dan Brown was German and wrote underwater sci-fi, this would be the result. <br/><br/>Bloated, cheesy, and filled with awfully written characters & tired old tropes - women defined by their looks, younger women in love with middle-aged men, and almost cartoonishly evil Americans. A lot of completely unnecessary romance & bizarrely placed introspection - case in point below:<br/><br/><i>He did feel scared and unsure of himself- more so with every step that took them closer to the hangar deck. What if there was nothi</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9372583191935907733" style="display:none">If Dan Brown was German and wrote underwater sci-fi, this would be the result. <br /><br />Bloated, cheesy, and filled with awfully written characters &amp; tired old tropes - women defined by their looks, younger women in love with middle-aged men, and almost cartoonishly evil Americans. A lot of completely unnecessary romance &amp; bizarrely placed introspection - case in point below:<br /><br /><i>He did feel scared and unsure of himself- more so with every step that took them closer to the hangar deck. What if there was nothing? By now he felt almost certain that they wouldn‚Äôt find a door, and then he‚Äôd have to get used to the idea that he might be delusional. He was fifty-six, he was good-looking, and people seemed to find him intelligent and charming. There was never any shortage of women.</i><br /><br />Exactly what his status an an obviously undeniable pussy-magnet has to do with him looking for a hidden door is beyond me. Like, seriously. I think my eyes almost rolled out of my head at that point - thankfully this was almost at the end of this 1,104 page behemoth, because by then I couldn't take much more.<br /><br />I am absolutely baffled as to how this made the 1001 list, other than perhaps they were trying to meet some kind of translated quota.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer478825297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer28832420137093424">Wow! What an achievement! The story begins with strange happenings in the ocean and its creatures, and takes a tremendous twist - the jacket cover description of an 'ecological thriller' is apt. So rather than talk about the story, I will talk about the writing.<br/><br/>The first thing that really struck me was the pace of the book. It begins as the events do - slow, sporadic, and almost lethargic. The reader is lulled into a feeling that strange things are happening, but they are going to take a long ti</span>
  <span id="freeText28832420137093424" style="display:none">Wow! What an achievement! The story begins with strange happenings in the ocean and its creatures, and takes a tremendous twist - the jacket cover description of an 'ecological thriller' is apt. So rather than talk about the story, I will talk about the writing.<br /><br />The first thing that really struck me was the pace of the book. It begins as the events do - slow, sporadic, and almost lethargic. The reader is lulled into a feeling that strange things are happening, but they are going to take a long time to unfold. However, Schaetzing uses simple devices such as changing sentence length and switching from one person's viewpoint to the next in order to control the pace of the action - and towards the end of the book, there really is a lot of action. I dare anyone to put the book down once they have begun the final third.<br /><br />Secondly, there are just so many characters and sub-plots in this book, most authors would have made a total mess of it. Here, the reader is drawn into the feeling, fears and lives of each and every major character in the book. Although it is difficult to empathize with so many characters in a single book, I did. And this is necessary for the intensity of the effect which events later on in the book have on the reader.<br /><br />Third, the book romanticizes nothing. Yes, there are messages about being eco-friendly, about the conspiracy theories of Americans (who are a bit too stereotyped in the book unfortunately), about how politics makes the world a mess, and so on, but the ending, to my surprise, was realistic and rather hard to deal with.<br /><br />And lastly, my goodness has he done his research or WHAT!! I am sure that there are hundreds of comments picking out all the mistakes that Schaetzing made in the book, but it is packed to the brim with information in a way which is incredibly easy to digest for a lay-person. To name a few, the reader learns about whales (not in general, but about how they migrate, feed, behave. About the difference in patterns between greys and orcas), ocean currents around the world (and why the gulf-stream is essential to us - cue 'An inconvenient truth'), the basics of DNA mutation theory, the culture, politics and geography of the Inuit, the biochemistry behind cellular life, the nature of continental shelves, the history of the continents, the chemistry and physics of methane, the policies and structures of the North Sea oil business, the nature of deep ocean exploration and why and how deep we have been, the philosophy of religion, the nature of mankind's belief in his supremacy, how deep-sea vents and volcanoes work, the biology and nature of various sea creatures, how ocean-going ships are structured and function .......and that is just the beginning.<br /><br />Oh, and lastly, the book was written in German, and this is a fabulous English translation. I would have never thought that it was not written in English. <br /><br />Buy it, read it, gift it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="28832420137093424" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/468387483" />
    <a title="Diego" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8008649-diego"><img alt="Diego" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471103207p2/8008649.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/468387483?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diego" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diego" href="/user/show/8008649-diego">Diego</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8008649-diego?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8008649-diego?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer468387483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11627644813794490976">This is a gripping book, and in his first part a thrilling one. The author spent several years to get all the scientific background that let him to write a convincing story. And he really achieved his objective, because the text results highly precise describing the stunning nature, at the same time it never loses pace.<br/><br/>The main focus of the plot is a world biological crisis, although the main characters don‚Äôt realize the magnitude of the events until the middle of the book. The story begins slow</span>
  <span id="freeText11627644813794490976" style="display:none">This is a gripping book, and in his first part a thrilling one. The author spent several years to get all the scientific background that let him to write a convincing story. And he really achieved his objective, because the text results highly precise describing the stunning nature, at the same time it never loses pace.<br /><br />The main focus of the plot is a world biological crisis, although the main characters don‚Äôt realize the magnitude of the events until the middle of the book. The story begins slowly and bewilder, and it gets faster while many strange events are related. The plot is set, in the first part of the book, in two distant coasts ‚ÄìBritish Columbia and Norway‚Äì, although there continuously occurs significant events all along the planet. In each one of these sceneries there are two scientists whose will be able to connect the dots and face the problem giving a call of alarm. These men are far from each other, not only geographically, but also in the intimacy of his personal history and their professional activities. However they are connected by the environment where they develop their work. This environment is the ocean ‚Äìwhere whales live, where oil extraction is done‚Äì and where will be waken the third main character in the novel, from which the book takes its name. <br /><br />The story is rather well developed, although the second part loses some pace, nevertheless it never becomes tame. All over the book you can feel the thrilling and breath-taking vertigo to try to catch the world as a whole, changing constantly the focus, and being impressed for the variety of stunning wild landscapes of our planet.<br /><br />In my opinion this novel is well worth reading, and I would recommend without hesitation.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11627644813794490976" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AbOSUw7gX426XbrW4EAHeGq709yZkYxAX4UZ8dX/sPIUPhVes/hZTvidueacOSnFkywdsVBCBxiD4r+/xsEQQg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XIek7sXsMLyho3hFdYiuMKDjTwJ7Gpu7mS1iGQcuUMdJCiPjePQ2f+Nje3UJ8YCNWXSBb7LJEONFSsRXFBDwdw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

What would the Earth look like from the sky, if we crossed the Mediterranean on the back of a goose? How did it feel to be a bee? How could we measure the speed of an insect‚Äôs wings and its heartbeat, or monitor its blood pressure and eating patterns?
